> ⟪ar:‏وَمَا هَذِهِ ٱلْحَيَوٰةُ ٱلدُّنْيَآ إِلَّا لَهْوٌۭ وَلَعِبٌۭ ۚ وَإِنَّ ٱلدَّارَ ٱلْآخِرَةَ لَهِىَ ٱلْحَيَوَانُ ۚ ‎⟫
> *And this life of the world is only amusement and play! Verily, the home of the Hereafter, that is the life indeed (i.e. the eternal life that will never end).*
> — **Quran 29:64**

Therefore, their dispositions remain ever connected to divine realities. When Maulana Ali was struck by the sword of Ibn Muljam, he instinctively exclaimed: "I have succeeded, by the Lord of the Kaʿbah!" At the moment of his passing, there remained only the remembrance of Allah; *lā ilāha illā-llāh* (there is no god but Allah) was upon his tongue. He died as he lived—upon the miracle. So did the Messenger of Allah teach: "The most blessed death is when one departs with one's tongue moist with the remembrance of Allah."


<!-- section 24 (id=5722, raw_sort=24): The Spirit Of The Intellect -->

## The Spirit of the Intellect

Our purpose here is to outline some of the key dimensions of the spiritual ethos of Mawlānā ʿAlī, with particular emphasis on its intellectual aspects. We shall demonstrate the pivotal role played by the intellect—*al-ʿAql* (the faculty of direct perception)—in the elaboration and assimilation of his spiritual teachings. A central aim is to elucidate what may be called the *spirit of the intellect* in Mawlānā ʿAlī's worldview: a spirit that not only "comprises" the operations of the rational mind but "surpasses" it, encompassing domains not customarily associated with intellection in modern discourse—domains such as moral comportment and aesthetic sensibility. This spiritual view of the intellect is directly related to the Qurʾānic concept of the spirit (*al-Rūḥ*, the animating principle); exploring its fruits reveals an integral vision wherein intellection and revelation converge.

A preliminary clarification proves useful regarding our choice of "intellect" over "reason" as the rendering of *ʿAql*. The term *intellect* evokes the original sense of the Latin *intellectus*: that faculty capable of direct contemplative vision of transcendent realities. Reason, by contrast, operates indirectly and discursively, working through logic to arrive at mental concepts of those realities. Through intellect, one contemplates or "sees" the Absolute; through reason alone, one can only think about it.

Three factors prove fundamental to this intellectual perspective. First, the centrality of revelation; second, the harmony between revelation and intellect; and third, the distinction between intellect (conceived as the principle articulating consciousness) and reason. Each requires careful consideration.

Regarding the first: everything in Mawlānā ʿAlī's life and teaching is centred upon the Qurʾānic revelation and its embodiment in the Prophet's conduct. Both his thought and his life may be read as a spiritual commentary—a *taʾwīl* (esoteric exegesis)—upon the sacred text. Doctrinally, Mawlānā ʿAlī possessed an "innate aptitude" for performing the task of *taʾwīl*. Existentially, his life unfolds as a dramatic manifestation of the inner meaning of revelation, from his birth within the sacred precincts of the Kaʿba to his martyrdom in the mosque of Kūfa. His refusal to remain at the superficial surface of things—whether in religion, ethics, or politics—manifested an unflinching fidelity to the sacred substance of faith, entailing both intellectual penetration inward and combative resistance outward.[^cite-63] The Prophet's saying carries decisive weight here: "ʿAlī shall fight for the *taʾwīl* of the Qurʾān as I fought for its *tanzīl* (its literal revelation)."[^cite-64]

Mawlānā ʿAlī's brief caliphate was dominated by civil wars. Yet it may be argued with confidence that all three were fought for the sake of correct interpretation and application of the Qurʾān, the clarification of its fundamental message (*tawḥīd*, the affirmation of divine unity), and the realization of this oneness on all spiritual and social planes. A poignant incident illustrates this principle. As the Battle of the Camel (*al-Jamal*) was about to commence, a Bedouin approached Mawlānā ʿAlī and asked, "O Commander of the Faithful! Do you say that Allāh is one?" Those present protested that this was neither the time nor place for such inquiry. Yet Mawlānā ʿAlī intervened: "Leave him, for surely what the Bedouin wishes is what we wish for the people."[^cite-65]

This response encapsulates a cardinal principle: right intention must take priority over outward action, whatever that action may be. For Mawlānā ʿAlī, the most fundamental intention motivating every action has been knowledge of the true nature of ultimate reality. Whether the context be religious in the conventional sense or military as here, what he "wishes for the people" is this knowledge, which must lie at the heart of all concerns, motivations, and actions—whether on the battlefield or the prayer-mat. Thus there is no time or place where the question "What do you mean by saying Allāh is one?" loses its relevance or propriety. It was for the sake of correct knowledge of the divine that Mawlānā ʿAlī waged these struggles, fulfilling thereby the prophecy of the Prophet.

Upon the field of battle itself, Mawlānā ʿAlī delivered a discourse on the meaning of divine unity. The opening of his reply provides a key to his entire spiritual ethos. He refutes the notion that Allāh's oneness is in any way "numerical" or "countable." One cannot speak of "one" while entertaining a numerical conception, for "that which has no second does not enter into the category of numbers."[^cite-66]

"That which has no second" signifies not merely a divinity to which no other partner may be ascribed, but that Reality which possesses no "otherness." *Al-Ḥaqq* (the Real)—simultaneously transcendent and immanent, beyond all "things" and manifest through all "things," at once inaccessible and inseparable—becomes disclosed through this utterance. In these few words, Mawlānā ʿAlī effects a shift of consciousness (for those who have "ears to hear"): from the dogmatic notion of one God versus many gods, to the mystical apprehension of the one and only Reality, before which all else appears as manifestation. All things become perceived as outward phenomena beckoning to be deciphered, as signs (*āyāt*, verses or marks) pointing to that of which they are manifestations. To retrace these appearances to their source and origin constitutes the literal meaning of *taʾwīl*: returning to *al-Awwal* (the First, the Origin).

The Qurʾān attests: *He is the First and the Last, the Outward and the Inward, and He is the All-Knower of every thing* (⟪quran 57:3⟫).[^cite-67] On the one hand, the Real is described as the beginning and end of all things, the apparent and the hidden; there exists nothing in being but this one Reality. On the other hand, apparent multiplicity must be "brought back" to real oneness. As Allāh declares: *We will show them Our Signs in the universe, and in their ownselves, until it becomes manifest to them that this is the truth* (⟪quran 41:53⟫).[^cite-68]

One saying attributed to Mawlānā ʿAlī references the Prophet as the first of those taught by Allāh the science of *taʾwīl*, with the Prophet then instructing Mawlānā ʿAlī. According to the Prophet: "Truly, ʿAlī is with the Qurʾān, and the Qurʾān is with ʿAlī." In another celebrated utterance, Mawlānā ʿAlī claimed capacity to fill seventy camels with commentarial pages on *Sūrat al-Fātiḥa* (the Opening Chapter). His cousin Ibn ʿAbbās likewise testified to having learned the science of interpretation from ʿAlī.

A saying of Mawlānā ʿAlī of paramount importance declares: "The Qurʾān consists of a book inscribed between two covers; it speaks not with a tongue, it cannot do without an interpreter (⟪ar:لا بُدَّا مِنَ التَّرجُمٰن⟫)."[^cite-69] This exchange between revelation and intellect finds further emphasis in another utterance: "The head of a man is the interpreter of his intellect (⟪ar:رَاسُ الرَّجَل تَرجُمان عَقلِہ⟫)."[^cite-70] Elsewhere, an explicit correspondence emerges between intellect and the "inner" messenger: the intellect is "the messenger of the Real" (*rasūl al-Ḥaqq*).

Thus Mawlānā ʿAlī's teachings—expressive of his spiritual ethos—should be read as creative interpretations of Qurʾānic revelation. They initiate a movement from the letters of revelation to its spirit, and from the spirit of revelation to the Spirit of the Revealer:

*Letters of Revelation* → *Spirit of Revelation* → *Revealer (al-Ḥaqq)*

This constitutes a process of "retracing" or returning to the source, the origin, *al-awwal*—accomplished to the measure that the spirit of the intellect is enlivened within the interpreter. Yet the Revealer remains inevitably concealed (as the essence of divine Reality) even as being revealed through revelation. The divine Reality in itself transcends all description in words; only the forms of its manifestations are susceptible to revelation. This dialectical interplay between form and formlessness, the evident and hidden, the revealed and concealed, and such antinomies lie at the root of the paradoxical mode of expression for which Mawlānā ʿAlī is renowned.

Indeed, one of the most striking aspects of Mawlānā ʿAlī's perspective is how it reveals the falsity of many dichotomies that later came to characterize or constrain Islamic thought. The alleged dichotomy between intellect and revelation manifests itself variously: reason versus faith, convention (*sunna*, custom) versus nature (*ṭabīʿa*), imitation (*taqlīd*) versus verification (*taḥqīq*), piety (*taqwa*) versus inquiry (*baḥth*, investigation). However, in Mawlānā ʿAlī's ethos, intellect and revelation, rather than being mutually exclusive, prove complementary and—at the highest level—indeed inseparable, for truth is one. While the human intellect cannot be equated with divine revelation, the spirit animating the intellect is not constrained by human limitations. It is this spirit which perceives and grasps the divine quality of revelation.[^cite-71]

The divine spirit articulating the form and infusing the content of revelation must be intelligible, to some degree, to human consciousness. Were it otherwise, revelation would reduce to mere rules and regulations for the will rather than serving as a source of creative inspiration for the intellect. Divine revelation presupposes creative engagement with the full resources of intellect, not the cessation of reason nor the imposition of unintelligible decrees. This is what the Qurʾān itself repeatedly calls for.

The revealed text is silent and "speaks" only through the "interpreter"—the intellect. This conception of the intellect-revelation relationship rigorously excludes all literalism and superficiality, all attempts to confine meaning within a flat, unilateral reading. Rather, such a view seeks the opposite: instead of "enclosing" meaning, it "dis-closes" it, opening the inner horizons of multiple levels of significance hidden within the text. This multivalent significance is explored in accordance with the depth and penetration of the interpreter's intellect. This "prophetic" intellect rejoins the source of revelation itself, for it is this "inner prophet," this innermost degree of consciousness, which forms the ultimate basis for affirming the verses of outward revelation as true. It "validates" them experientially, not merely affirms them dogmatically. Thus Mawlānā ʿAlī states in the first sermon of the *Nahj al-Balāgha* that one purpose of revelation—of "the sending forth of prophets" by Allāh to humankind—was "to unearth for them the buried treasures of the intellects (⟪ar:دفائن العقول⟫)."[^cite-72]

This saying constitutes a key to understanding his spiritual ethos: the intellect is not stifled by revelation; rather, it is enlivened by it. Revelation is a potent summons to the spiritual depths of intellect, cultivating and bringing to fruition the seeds of consciousness implanted therein by Allāh. The spirit of one's intellect, then, cannot be alien to that which descends as revelation from Allāh. It must be fundamentally akin to it. For precisely this reason, one may have absolute certainty that revelation indeed comes from the Absolute. The question is not one of setting up a dichotomy between intellect or revelation, but of perceiving intellect *as* a mode of revelation—of seeing it as the God-given means by which the individual discerns, affirms, and plumbs the divine "revelations" external to oneself: both the "signs" (*āyāt*) of scripture and those constituted by the natural world. If human intellect can perceive and recognize revelation, this occurs because both are rooted in the same ultimate source. Prophetic revelation awakens and enlivens the depths of human intellect; it does not replace or displace it. From this perspective, the revealed text is not to be viewed as extraneous to intelligence, opposed to it, but rather as the objective, outward expression of the very principles that intellect itself yields to the "inner prophet." By "intellect" one must therefore understand not simply the faculty of reasoning, but the source of consciousness articulating the human spirit.

The intellect contains unsuspected depths—"buried treasures"—and is thus far from being exhausted by its surface operations or by the cognitions and reasonings proper to its purely mental dimension. These reflections return us to the relationship between intellect and reason. Just as there can be no contradiction between *revelation* and *intellect*, likewise one must avoid erecting a false dichotomy between *reason* and *intellect*. Instead, reason should be understood as one mode of the intellect. Reason does not contradict intellect any more than intellect contradicts revelation. Reason constitutes a mode of intellect but is not to be equated with it. Likewise, one cannot reduce the universal objectivity of revelation to the subjective particularities of any individual's intellect or to one's "private" revelation. To equate reason with intellect would amount to denying the existence of the "buried treasures" of intellect—those dimensions transcending rationality and initially "buried" beneath, hence hidden from, the rational surface. Revelation constituted by intellect is embedded within human creation. It is this intellect which most critically defines humanity as such.

Rather than setting intellect against revelation, each must be seen in the light of the other. The substance of revelation cannot be engaged except through intellect; conversely, the "buried treasures" of intellect cannot be disclosed except through revelation. This understanding of intellect draws us near to what Mawlānā ʿAlī designates as the "heart," that innermost mode of perception capable of "seeing" Allāh. It is the heart that "sees" Allāh—not through outward vision but through "the realities of faith" (*ḥaqāʾiq al-īmān*), the spiritual insight generated by true principles of belief. As Mawlānā ʿAlī responds to one who asked whether he had "seen" his Lord: "Eyes see Him not through sight's observation, but hearts see Him through the true principles and beliefs of faith."[^cite-73]

Returning to the relationship between intellect and reason: between the spiritual depths of intellect and its rational surface there exists both continuity and discontinuity. Continuity manifests in the evident fact that all thinking begins with intelligible concepts. Thought initially concerns itself with rationally discernible ideas that must bear some relation to the realities they express. Yet discontinuity also obtains: reason halts at ideas that are "de-fined." The Arabic word for definition, *ḥadd* (the upper limit), simultaneously expresses both conceptual definition and the sense of a boundary, a limitation. To transcend the limits imposed by rational definition requires something beyond reason itself. This "something more" constitutes what makes this ethos spiritual rather than merely rationalistic. The ethos is indeed "rational" in that its form is susceptible to reason's scrutiny, but it is not "rationalistic" insofar as its essence is not predicated upon reason alone.

Mawlānā ʿAlī frequently alludes—in highly allusive language—to the mysteries and marvels (the unearthed treasures of intellect) that he has directly witnessed through the eye of his purified heart. He indirectly evokes this supra-rational awareness through the bewildering deployment of apophatic discourse (knowledge of God obtained through negation) and paradoxical utterances, abundantly found in the works attributed to him. In this ethos, the higher the truth, the deeper the paradox. The incommensurability between reality-in-itself and human conceptions of it is effectively evoked through this creative use of paradox. Each human conception requires first to be "deconstructed" so that it may then be "reconstructed." Mawlānā ʿAlī's paradoxes feed directly into modes of spiritual intuition, contemplation, and realization—modes which reason alone cannot attain. This intuition and contemplation "re-member" or "reconstruct" the spiritual realities transcending the categories of reason. The value of paradoxes, antinomies (contradictions between reasonable conclusions), apophasis, and negation is entirely dependent upon the positive realities which intellect can plumb, thereby becoming aware of its own hidden depths. The resulting consciousness does not negate reason so much as build upon it, revealing the limits of reason's proper sphere. It demonstrates that reason alone cannot "comprehend" or fully express higher realities, however indispensable it may be in initiating the cognitive movement toward them.

Thus, when Mawlānā ʿAlī negates the possibility of human conceptions encompassing divine Reality, what is negated is the limitation that inevitably accompanies all such conceptions—not the positive content, the *maʿnā* (spiritual meaning), of the conception itself. This conception serves as a necessary starting-point for a process of assimilation engaging a deeper level of awareness than that "defined" by the mind, extending far beyond speculative thought. In this light, the negation of the limitations of human conception should be seen as introducing absolute affirmation: the affirmation of the Absolute, which knows no limitation. The spiritual negation of conceptual limitation operates in an upward, "constructive" order, not a nihilistic, deconstructive one. Nowhere is this style more apparent than in the celebrated opening of the first sermon of the *Nahj al-Balāgha*:

> Praise be to God, whose praise those who speak cannot deliver, whose graces those who count cannot number, whose rightful due those who strive cannot render; He who cannot be grasped by aspirations, nor fathomed by profound intuitions; He whose attribute has no binding limitation, no existing description, no time appointed, no term extended.

The divine attributes (*ṣifāt*, qualities) cannot be confined within a definition (*laysa li-ṣifātihī ḥaddun maḥdūd*),[^cite-74] and yet they are affirmed as real. They are described (*mawṣūf*) by the very words of Qurʾānic revelation, even though the divine nature has "no description." Allāh is described as possessing attributes—the Merciful (*al-Raḥmān*), the Compassionate (*al-Raḥīm*), the Almighty (*al-ʿAzīz*)—yet Mawlānā ʿAlī affirms that these very attributes cannot be defined, bounded, or circumscribed. The paradox sharpens further when he speaks of the perfection of *ikhlāṣ* (rendering sincere, the purification of devotion to Allāh and conception of Him)—which consists in divesting (*nafyī*, negation) Allāh of all attributes. On the one hand, the attributes are affirmed; on the other, they are negated. The rational mind can only conceive Allāh through the attributes verbally expressed in the Qurʾān. Yet here, Mawlānā ʿAlī appears to negate these same attributes, thereby depriving the mind of any convenient conceptual grasp of divine Reality. This simultaneous affirmation and negation of the attributes raises an apparently irresolvable contradiction. Yet in doing so, it heightens receptivity to a deeper mode of knowing. This style of discourse awakens a latent awareness inscribed in the heart—in the depths of intellect. This awareness then invites the seeker to "dive" into the knowledge "buried" within intellect. The very shock of apparent contradiction precipitates a leap of consciousness from the formal activity of reason to the super-rational contemplation of the spirit.

Consider the words Mawlānā ʿAlī spoke immediately after affirming that his heart had indeed witnessed Allāh—not through outward vision but through "the realities of faith":

> My Lord is not described in terms of dimension (⟪ar:بُعْد⟫), nor motion (⟪ar:حرکۃ⟫), nor rest (⟪ar:سکون⟫), nor through coming or going. He is subtle in subtlety but not described by subtlety. He is magnificent in magnificence but not described by magnificence. He is great in greatness but not described by greatness.[^cite-75]

The attributes are again affirmed and denied. Allāh possesses these qualities, yet is not reducible to them. One may begin to think of Allāh through such categories, but cannot reduce Him to one's limited conceptions. The attributes surely provide the mind with something by which divine nature may be understood; yet this understanding is fragmentary and never exhaustive. It therefore requires negation, so that fuller understanding may emerge. The initial conceptual orientation to divine Reality must be both negated and sublimated: negated as mental conception, while its positive content is elevated to a higher rank through spiritual intuition. Only thus can the two-dimensional "portrait" defined by reason receive the third dimension of depth. But this third dimension emerges only when the insufficiency of rational depiction is clearly perceived. Paradox produces the cognitive shock that reveals reason's presumption in attempting to disclose the nature of the Absolute.

One discerns in Mawlānā ʿAlī's discourses a repeated combination of affirmation and denial, which mirrors the negation and affirmation found in Islam's first testimony: *Lā ilāha* ("No god")—the negation (*nafyī*); then *Illā Allāh* ("except Allāh")—the affirmation (*isbāt*). This illuminates how the perfection of *ikhlāṣ*, as described by Mawlānā ʿAlī, is attained through the negation of the attributes "in order to" affirm the infinitude of divine Reality. Thus each attribute must be "negated," but only insofar as it is conceived by the limited mind. Negation proves necessary because at the mental level, each attribute "excludes" not only the other attributes but also the object of attribution itself (the divine Essence). For any concept to develop in the mind, defined ideas are required. Wherever there are definitions, there are limits, which result in exclusions. However, the reality of the attribute is unlimited by virtue of its absolute identity with the Essence. The attribute that has been "negated" is paradoxically affirmed as being nothing "but" Allāh: *Illā Allāh*. Thus one can place each negated attribute in the position of the negation (*nafyī*) of the first testimony, arriving at such formulations as: *Lā Raḥmāna illā-Allāh* ("There is no All-Compassionate except Allāh"); *Lā Raḥīma illā-Allāh* ("There is no All-Merciful except Allāh").

In this manner, the apparent negation of the attributes becomes an affirmation of their true identity, simultaneously disconnecting any restrictive association with the poverty of mental conceptions. The attributes are not superimposed upon the Essence; they are in fact absolutely one with it. The attributes are differentiable from the Essence only in conception, not in existence. The Essence indeed comprises all mentioned attributes—greatness, subtlety, and the like—yet it is infinitely more than the sum of these qualities. These attributes are infinite only within and as the Essence. Mawlānā ʿAlī's explanation of this paradox may itself be read as a commentary upon the Qurʾānic verse: *Glorified be Allah above all that they attribute to Him!* (⟪quran 23:91⟫).[^cite-76]

The infinitude of Allāh requires the negation of all that the mind can conceive through reason on the human plane. Yet it does not negate all that the heart can "see" through spiritual vision. This very negation on the plane of reason summons something deeper. This in turn can be understood only in the light of spiritual practice—always the reflective, devotional, or contemplative context for the hearers of Mawlānā ʿAlī's sermons and discourses. The heart, which is the symbolic seat of contemplative intelligence, calls out to be "polished" (*tajlīl*), and this is precisely what the remembrance of Allāh accomplishes. This is why, according to Mawlānā ʿAlī, the remembrance of Allāh (*Dhikr Allāh*) constitutes "the most excellent form of worship."[^cite-77]

Interestingly, even at the level of practice, a further paradox emerges. For the "reality of remembrance" is not attained until "you forget yourself" in your remembrance of Allāh. It is not merely the rational mind and its concepts that must be negated or "deconstructed," but the individual "soul" itself—the locus of all cognition, rational or supra-rational—that must be negated or "deconstructed." This final annihilation (*fanāʾ*, the dissolution of the individual self in the Absolute), constitutes the ultimate form of paradox.

In Mawlānā ʿAlī's perspective, the intellect must be understood, first and foremost, as a spiritual faculty. And the spirit must be appreciated as not only the source of intellect, but also the source of life. Without intellect, the spirit is reduced to an unconscious existence. Conversely, without the spirit, intellect tends toward lifeless thoughts. This is why the Qurʾān, describing the creation of man, states that Allāh breathed His spirit into Adam:

*Who made everything He has created good, and He began the creation of man from clay. Then He made his offspring from semen of worthless water. Then He fashioned him in due proportion, and breathed into him the soul, and He gave you hearing, sight and hearts. Little is the thanks you give!* (⟪quran 32:7-9⟫).[^cite-78]

The following passage from the first sermon of the *Nahj al-Balāgha* comments extensively upon this infusion of divine spirit into humanity:

> Then Allāh (may He be glorified), gathered up a quantity of dust, from some hard earth and some soft, some sweet earth and some salty, and poured water onto it until it was pure, then kneaded into it some moisture until it stuck together. He then fashioned from this, a form with curves and joints, limbs and parts. He compacted it until it held tightly together. He solidified it until it became as sounding clay, assigning to it a measured duration and a known consummation. Then He breathed into it of His spirit, and it stood up as a human being, endowed with intellectual powers with which to reflect, thoughts by which to conduct himself, limbs to put to service, instruments at his disposal, and knowledge with which to discern between the true and the false.[^cite-79]

In other words, it is the "in-spiration" (literally, the "in-breathing" and the insertion of the spirit) of Allāh's breath that produces consciousness and life. Death, conversely, constitutes "ex-piration," the departure of the spirit. Life, in the absence of fidelity to one's spirit, becomes a kind of death. Through this spirit, inanimate, unconscious matter is brought to life and granted awareness of itself. It generates not only knowledge of other forms but also knowledge of the self. This intimate intertwining of the gift of life with the capacity to know—existence which is conscious, knowledge which is existential—forms one of the foundations of any truly "spiritual" perspective. The question is not one of contrasting spirit with body, but of recognizing that the infusion of spirit "into" the body brings the body to life. Thus "spiritual" knowledge enlivens the hearts:

*O you who believe! Answer Allāh and His messenger when he calls you to that which will give you life, and know that Allāh comes in between a person and his heart. And verily to Him you shall be gathered.* (⟪quran 8:24⟫).[^cite-80]

According to Mawlānā ʿAlī, one of the most significant aspects of this "call" from Allāh to humanity is to "remind" them of the buried treasures of the intellect.[^cite-81]

Existence is the bestowal of conscious spirit upon the body. Hence the *ʿAql* (intellect), which is the principle articulating the spirit, is not merely a mental faculty but constitutes the very foundation of our true life. Mental faculty represents only one mode of intellect's operation, but far from the only one. According to the sayings of Mawlānā ʿAlī, the *ʿAql* is multifaceted, comprising aspects that in our times are rarely associated with intelligence.

To be "spiritual," then, means to remain true to one's intellect—to the treasures buried deep within it—rather than merely to the rational functions operating at its surface. For Mawlānā ʿAlī, the "true intellectual" (*al-ʿāqil*) is one who not only "thinks" correctly but also "acts" ethically and, at the deepest level, seeks to "realize" ultimate Reality. He defines the intellectual as "one who puts all things in their proper place." Because this constitutes the very definition of justice also, only the true intellectual can be fully "just." Only one who possesses the ability to see things as they "truly are" can place them in their right place. Therefore, thought, action, and realization are all concerns of the true intellectual—the *ʿāqil*. Correct thinking, impeccable virtue, and authentic being are inseparably bound to the intellect in this spiritual ethos. These dimensions become clarified through Mawlānā's discourse to Kumayl, to which we now turn our attention.

<!-- section 25 (id=5713, raw_sort=25): The Discourse To Kumayl -->

## The Discourse to Kumayl

This discourse has long commanded the attention of Islamic scholars and spiritual interpreters. Its significance rests fundamentally upon its profoundly esoteric character—a quality that renders it particularly instructive for those who seek to penetrate the inner dimensions of spiritual knowledge.

The account relates how Maulana Ali mysteriously took one of his most devoted disciples, Kumayl ibn Ziyad, outside the city of Kufa to a graveyard, where he imparted a counsel of extraordinary spiritual import. There, surrounded by the silent testimony of the dead, he addressed Kumayl thus:

> O Kumayl ibn Ziyad, truly these hearts are vessels, and the best of them are those which hold the most. So retain from me that which I say to you. People are divided into three types: a lordly knower, one who seeks knowledge (⟪ar:متعلم⟫) for the sake of deliverance; and the common folk following just anyone, swaying with every current, not desiring to be illumined by the light of knowledge, nor seeking refuge from any strong support.
>
> O Kumayl, knowledge is better than wealth, for knowledge guards you, while you must guard wealth; and wealth diminishes as it is spent, while knowledge increases as it is disbursed; and the results of wealth disappear with the disappearance of wealth. O Kumayl ibn Ziyad, the wise apprehension of knowledge is a religion by which (Allah) is worshipped. Through it, man acquires obedience in his life and a good name after his death. And knowledge is a judge, while wealth is judged.
>
> O Kumayl ibn Ziyad, those who hoard wealth perish even while they live, but the knowers endure for as long as time subsists; their (material) forms are absent, but their (spiritual) images in the hearts are present. Ah! what abundant knowledge is here (pointing to his breast); If only I could find one to bear it. Yes, I found one who was quick to understand, but he could not be trusted with it, exploiting the tools of religion for the sake of worldly gain, empowering himself with the graces of Allah against His slaves, and with His proofs against His friends. Then there was one who was obedient to the bearers of truth, but lacking heartfelt insight; at the first appearance of obscurity, doubt was kindled in his heart. So alas, neither this one nor that! Nor one who is greedy for pleasure, submissive to passion, nor one obsessed with acquisition and accumulation—neither of them is a guardian of religion in any respect. They resemble nothing so much as grazing cattle! Thus does knowledge die with the death of its bearers.
>
> But indeed! By Allah, the earth will never be empty of one (Imam) who establishes the proof of Allah whether overtly with publicity or fearfully in obscurity, lest Allah's proofs and elucidations come to naught. But such as these—how many are they and where? By Allah, they may be the smallest in number, but with Allah they are the greatest in rank. Through them Allah preserves His proofs and elucidations, so that they entrust them to their compeers and sow them in the hearts of those resembling them. Through them, knowledge penetrates the reality of insight. They rejoice in their intimacy with the spirit of certainty; they make easy what the extravagant find harsh; they befriend that by which the ignorant are estranged. With their bodies they keep company with the world, while their spirits are tied to the transcendent realm. They are the vicegerents of Allah on His earth summoners to His religion. Ah! how I long to see them! Go now, Kumayl, as you will.

---

## The "Lordly Knower" and the Preservation of Religion

For our purposes here, it suffices to reflect upon the four sentences in this discourse that offer us the description of those who possess the station of 'lordly knower' (⟪ar:فَعَالِمٌ رَبَّانِيٌّ⟫)—those who are fewest in number yet greatest in the sight of Allah and through whom 'His religion' is sustained.[^cite-82] His religion, that is, denotes the divine reality of religion itself, the pure essence of the faith, which is perfect and immutable, in sharp contrast to 'religion' understood in its ordinary sense as the formal institutions subject to the vicissitudes of human history. It is by means of the knowledge of the true knowers (al-ʿārifūn, those possessing direct knowledge) that religion remains true to itself, and it is only through such sages that religion truly 'lives' and manifests itself in the world.[^cite-83]

## Through Them, Knowledge Penetrates the Reality of Insight

As noted before, knowledge can remain merely abstract and far removed from the 'reality of insight'—separated from lived experience and spiritual actualization. Yet knowledge contains within itself a potential that calls out to be realized. It needs to be enlivened in order to 'penetrate' into something deeper than the words and concepts that articulate its own formal expression—deeper than the immediately intelligible meaning on the surface. Degrees of knowledge are clearly implied here, along with an emphatic rejection of all literalism and superficiality, the facile equation of factual information with true knowledge, let alone wisdom. The shift of consciousness required for the 'penetration' of knowledge is indicated graphically by Maulana Ali's gesture of pointing to his breast.[^cite-84]

The Arabic word *hajama*—rendered here as 'penetrate'—carries in its primary meaning the sense of 'to assault,' 'to take by storm,' 'to charge forward with force.' In this context, the word reveals to us that within conceptual knowledge there resides a latent force—one might almost say an 'explosive' power—deriving from the very reality of knowledge, its *ḥaqīqa* (spiritual essence). It is this inner force that calls out to be 'made real' through *taḥaqqūq* (investigation and realization in depth), not merely through factual verification on the surface. This *ḥaqīqa* can be indicated but never fully captured by the words and concepts which constitute its formal, communicable aspect. By means of this inner force—which its outer conceptual form both conceals and evokes—knowledge becomes capable of effecting a profound shift of consciousness, from one degree of understanding to another.

An indirect, mental conception of reality thus paves the way for a 'penetrating,' existential perception of reality. The 'reality of insight' (*ḥaqīqat al-baṣīra*) opens the intelligence to reality as such, whereas ordinary 'insight,' like abstract knowledge alone, remains confined to the superficial level of understanding. The same idea appears in a saying of Maulana Ali relating to true understanding, which must plumb and thus truly 'know' the depths of knowledge, its own deeper dimensions or 'buried treasures': 'He who understands, knows the depth of knowledge' (⟪ar:من فھم علم گوہر العلم⟫).[^cite-85] The effort required to attain true knowledge, as opposed to mere accumulation of facts, is likewise indicated in this saying: 'Through learning and assimilating knowledge [such that it becomes deeply rooted in one's consciousness], knowledge is attained' (⟪ar:بالتَّعَلُّم یَنَالُ العِلمْ⟫).

The urgency of realizing the immanent power within knowledge—of moving from the abstract to the concrete, from potential to actual, from the static to the dynamic, from the formal to the essential—is effectively evoked by the use of the word *hajama*, which resonates with the imagery of battle. It alerts one to the grave dangers of mistaking the initial understanding of an idea for its full comprehension, and the intellectual comprehension of it for its dynamic spiritualization and interiorization. This interiorization is a process of infinite unfolding within, not the simple registering of some external fact. It thus demands the effective integration of knowledge within being; knowledge from without must be transformed into being from within, woven into the deepest texture of one's existence. Thus, knowledge demands not merely the attention of the mind but the total orientation of the soul.

In this ethos, the effective integration of knowledge within being—what might be called a living *tawhīd* (the realization of divine unity)—is unattainable without the participation of the whole soul. This, in turn, requires the elimination of all vices that flow from egocentricity. Thus, the *ʿāqil* (the true intellectual), is described by Maulana Ali as being constantly engaged in 'struggle against his own soul'—against the lower inclinations, the passionate whims, and distracting caprices of the ego.[^cite-86] All these vices, far from being confined to the moral plane, encroach upon the domain of knowledge itself; this will be understood in the very measure that knowledge is grasped as a concrete vision of things, and not merely as a notional or conceptual understanding. The vices are thus regarded as so many clouds obscuring the vision proper to the 'reality of insight.' According to the Quran, evil actions become 'rust' that covers over the heart:

⟪quran 83:14⟫ Nay! But on their hearts is the *Ran* which they used to earn.

The *ʿāqil* (the intellectual), then, is engaged in constant inner struggle against the tendencies which not only sully the soul but also blind the heart. As a virtuous person, the *ʿāqil* opposes vice on the moral plane because it contradicts virtue. But vice is opposed for yet another reason—one situated at the higher level of spiritual reality. It contradicts the nature of the Real; it obscures the reality of insight, blinding the 'eye' of the heart which alone can 'see' Allah.

It is not a question here of denying the validity of the moral perspective; rather, the stress falls upon the deeper motivation that propels the *ʿāqil* toward virtue. This motivation specifically transcends the moral plane while comprising it; morality emerges here as the fruit or effect, spirituality as the seed or cause. By drawing attention to the intellectual aspect of the *ʿāqil*'s motivation for being rid of vices, Maulana Ali brings out more clearly the way in which immorality is rooted in ignorance. The uprooting of this ignorance, for the true intellectual, is thus tantamount to dissolving the very substance of the vices. This uprooting requires great effort, a relentless battle within, which is indeed the most formidable of all battles—*al-jihād al-akbar* (the greatest struggle), as it was defined by the Prophet.

One should take careful note of the following sayings of Maulana Ali on the importance of this inner struggle:

- Struggling against the soul through knowledge—such is the mark of the intellect.
- The strongest people are those who are strongest against their own souls.
- Truly, one who fights his own soul, in obedience to Allah and does not sin against Him, has the rank of the righteous martyr in Allah's eyes.
- The ultimate battle is that of a man against his own soul.
- He who knows his soul fights it.
- No jihad is more excellent than the jihad of the soul.

In another saying, Maulana Ali clarifies what the Prophet meant by defining the greatest jihad as that of the soul (*al-nafs*). It is not so much a question of fighting against the soul but rather the battle **for** the soul itself. This emerges from the metaphor the Imam employs to define the struggle: *al-ʿAql* (the Intellect) is the leader of the forces of *al-Raḥmān* (the All-Compassionate); *al-hawā* (whim, caprice, desire) commands the forces of *al-shayṭān* (the devil). The soul (*al-nafs*) vacillates between them, susceptible to the attraction of both (⟪ar:مُتَجَاذِبَہ بینھما⟫), and enters into 'the domain' of whichever of the two will triumph.

These sayings underline the fact that, for the true intellectual, the soul in its entirety must be 'conquered' and enlisted on the side of the Intellect. It is not a question of destroying the lower soul but of ridding it of the destructive influence of its caprice, its egoistic whims, subjectivism and individualism—all notions connoted by the word *hawā*. The Prophet, we are told in the Quran, speaks not from *hawā*; rather:

⟪quran 53:3-4⟫ And what is spoken of God is nothing but revelation / Nor does he speak of desire. It is only an Inspiration that is inspired.

The divine objectivity of revelation is here contrasted with the human subjectivity of passional whim. In the context of the holy war within, an analogous polarity is brought into focus by Maulana Ali's sayings. The objectivity of the Intellect must prevail against the subjectivity of individual caprice and egotistic desire. It is the Intellect that brings to the forefront the divine power of *al-Raḥmān* and that combats the forces of *al-shayṭān*, represented by *al-hawā*.

The word *hawā* is further used in the Qur'an in the sense of the desires stemming from the ego, by means of which the ego becomes a kind of false god:

⟪quran 25:43⟫ Have you seen him who has taken as his deity his own desire? Would you then be a witness over him?

This is the subtle or hidden idolatry (⟪ar:الشرک الخفی⟫) that the Prophet referred to as being more hidden than a black ant crawling on a black stone in a moonless night.[^cite-87] To eliminate this idolatry of the ego, some force higher than that of the ego must be engaged, as it is impossible for the ego to be controlled or dominated by something pertaining to the ego itself. This higher force is the divine Reality, access to which is made possible through the power of the Intellect and the graces of faith. This complementarity between the effort to overcome the egoism of *hawā* and the influx of divine grace is well expressed by the following verse of the Qur'an:

⟪quran 79:40-41⟫ But as for him who feared standing before his Lord, and restrained himself from impure evil desires, / Verily, Paradise will be his abode.

The soul's fundamental energy is not to be destroyed but converted and redirected away from the transient objects of individualistic desire, away from the impulses of *al-shayṭān*, toward the one, true object that is expressed by *al-Raḥmān*. This 'conquest' of the soul involves much more than attaining to some mental conception. If one is to be rendered capable of perceiving that truth in all its dimensions and ramifications on the different planes of being, then it is not only the mind, the will and the character, but the fundamental disposition of the soul that needs to be integrated into the truth perceived by the Intellect.

## Tawhīd: Realizing the One

It is important here to stress that, for Maulana Ali, the need to win the soul over in its totality follows naturally from his understanding of *Tawhīd* (the realization and affirmation of divine unity), the most fundamental principle of Islam. It is crucial not to lose sight of the fact that *Tawhīd* is both a noun and a verb. It is often simply translated as 'oneness' or 'unity,' but it literally means 'affirming one' (the affirmation of and belief in the oneness of Allah—the outward, theological meaning), or 'realizing the One' (the realization that there is **but** one reality—the inner, spiritual meaning). Thus, in Maulana Ali's perspective, the Absolutely Real is perceived not only as being One but also as making one, integrating. The Real is not only unique; it is also unifying. *Tawhīd*, then, comes to mean not merely affirming the oneness of Allah but also "being" one in order to reflect perfectly the One. To know Allah is to be 'integrated.' One observes here a link with the etymology of the word *ʿaqal*, whose primary meaning is 'to bind or restrain.' Whereas for the later mystical tradition the limitative aspect of the word is stressed—the Intellect being that which defines and encloses meaning, as opposed to the heart which intuits and discloses spiritual truth—here one sees that the Intellect is that which 'binds together' the disparate elements of the soul into an organic unity. This principle is expressed in the first of the following series of Maulana Ali's sayings, each of which merits deep reflection:

- He who knows Allah integrates himself (⟪ar-quote:مَنْ عَرَفَ اللہ تَوَحَّدُ⟫)
- He who knows his soul disengages himself
- He who knows people isolates himself
- He who knows the world withholds himself

Elsewhere, this total adherence to the Real is referred to by Maulana Ali as 'sincerity,' *ṣidq* or *ikhlāṣ*—terms which denote far more than simply truthfulness, as is already evident from the root of the word *ikhlāṣ*. The word *khaluṣa* means to be utterly pure, true to one's essence, unadulterated by any extraneous substance. This aspect of the meaning is well expressed in the following saying of Maulana Ali:

⟪ar-quote:أخلص لی اللہ العلم و العمل والحب و العداوۃ، والقبض و المغادرۃ، والنطق و الصمت⟫

'Make purely for Allah your action and your knowledge, your love and your hatred, your taking and your leaving, your speech and your silence.'

To be totally sincere, then, means to make oneself 'purely' for Allah, to have no 'stain' of impurity—here understood as what is other than Allah—present in one's devotion, action, intention and even sentiment. One must love and hate only in function of one's relationship with Allah. Of all the tendencies which insinuate themselves within one's motivations and intentions, the most pernicious is egotism. *Ikhlāṣ*, pure sincerity, involves the active effort to BE, THINK and ACT purely for Allah, that is, with selflessness.[^cite-88] Hence the stress placed by Maulana Ali on the holy war within the soul as the 'ultimate battle.' One who succeeds in this battle attains the rank of the 'righteous martyr.' The victor here is the martyr (*shahīd*), because he has sacrificed his lower life for the sake of Allah with whom is the real life, that which is witnessed by the Intellect—the only element within human consciousness capable of objectively looking upon the soul as 'the other.'

To repeat: 'Struggling against the soul through knowledge such is the mark of the Intellect.' The intellectual in this battle is also one who bears 'witness' (*al-shahīd*, meaning both martyr and witness, as in the original Greek meaning of 'martyr'). He bears witness to Allah through the Intellect's sacrifice of that which opposes the truth of Allah, and the key opponent in question is his own egotism, his own 'false life.' To have perfect *ikhlāṣ*, then, is to be rid of all hypocritical self-preoccupation and to install in its place a sincere theocentricity, a mode of being 'centered' upon Allah, not oneself. Thus, *ikhlāṣ* is to be for Allah or to belong to Allah, in the sense indicated by the Quranic verse:

⟪quran 6:162⟫ Say, "My prayers, my asceticism, my life and my death are for Allah, the Lord of the worlds."

The fact that worship is the first thing mentioned in this verse is of particular significance for anyone wishing to appreciate the nature of Maulana Ali's spiritual ethos (⟪ar:مزاج⟫). What must be stressed in the presentation of this ethos is the indispensable role performed by worship and devotion. One of the key distinguishing features of Maulana Ali's spiritual ethos, indeed, is the stress he places on prayer, taking this term in its widest sense to include all those means—from formal worship and supplication to meditation and reflection, remembrance and contemplation—by which the individual is oriented and attuned to the Real, *al-Ḥaqq*.[^cite-89]

Worship is, therefore, an integral aspect of *ikhlāṣ*, in the very measure that worship relates to and sustains a fundamental dimension of the human soul. That within the soul which yearns to worship and is satisfied only by the sustenance given through worship must be given its due. In a spiritual context, if worship is marginalized, one's sincerity is compromised, for one is not being sincere to Allah, not being 'just' to Allah—not rendering Allah His due. And the spiritual logic of Maulana Ali dictates that if one is not just with Allah, one cannot be just to His creatures. He says: 'Whoever establishes well-being between himself and Allah, Allah establishes well-being between him and mankind.'

One might also quote here the verse of the Quran:

⟪quran 15:99⟫ And worship your Lord until certainty comes to you.

The quest for knowledge, then, demands total dedication if it is to yield up its inner riches, and this dedication includes that transformation of consciousness shaped by total concentration on the divine Reality—prayer in one of its deepest modes. In this form of prayer, the intensity of 'dedication' is such that there is no room in one's consciousness for anything other than the object to which one is dedicated—the 'remembrance' (⟪ar:ذکر⟫) of Allah strictly implying the forgetting of everything else, including, preeminently, oneself. As mentioned earlier, the spiritual reality of *dhikr* can only be attained when 'you forget your soul in your remembrance.' Concentration upon the divine Reality as the supreme object of knowledge, rendered present through the Name or Names of Allah, is thus a mode of prayer. And for it to be effective, it requires both presence of mind and a serious engagement of the will. It is far from sufficient to pray absentmindedly in mechanical fashion. In this connection, Maulana Ali says, 'He who prays without exertion is like one who shoots arrows without a bow.' Likewise, there is certainly nothing passive or easy about the process of acquiring knowledge: 'Knowledge calls out for action; if it is answered, it is of avail; otherwise it departs.'

The same idea is expressed by Maulana Ali more forcefully in this saying: 'When you hear knowledge being imparted, cleave to it; do not mix it up with jest, lest hearts spew it forth.' Knowledge that is not taken seriously will be of no avail. It remains 'undigested' and refuses to be assimilated by the 'heart,' the faculty that has access to the 'reality of insight' and which can thus 'see' Allah. In fact, it is not the individual who 'assimilates' knowledge to himself, but knowledge—or rather, the **reality of being** expressed by true knowledge—that assimilates to itself the individual: 'Acquire knowledge, and true life will acquire you.' The relationship between life and consciousness, so central to the intrinsic function of the spirit, is again being stressed in this maxim. At first, knowledge is sought by the individual and consciousness is cultivated; then, life 'seeks' the individual as his being makes itself 'real' or realizes itself more fully within him. In other words, the individual first makes an active effort to 'integrate' knowledge within his being; but at a certain point, this activity is transformed into passive receptivity—being, 'true life,' integrates the individual within itself. To return to Maulana Ali's guidance to Kumayl: 'Through them knowledge penetrates the reality of insight.' It is not the knowers who penetrate; it is knowledge that penetrates into the reality of insight, doing so through them, and this in the very measure of their effacement within knowledge—an effacement which is the shadow cast by their effective integration within the life that 'acquires' them.

## They Rejoice in Their Intimacy with the Spirit of Certainty

The next sentence in the discourse to Kumayl is of particular importance in helping us to appreciate the sheer happiness that flows from the 'life of the spirit.' In the Arabic expression ⟪ar:وبشِّرُ روح الیقین⟫, the primary meaning of the word *bashara* means having intimate experience of a thing, coming into direct physical contact with it. This form of the verb also relates to joy. Hence, the phrase 'rejoice in intimate contact' better conveys something of the meaning of this single word. To touch the 'spirit of certainty' is to come into contact, not with some cold logical fact, the conclusion of a purely mental process. Rather, this contact with the spirit of certainty means engaging deeply with the object of certainty, with that of which one is certain—ultimately, this being nothing less than the divine Reality itself. Since this Reality is infinitely more beatific than any conceivable paradisal bliss—according to the Quran, the divine *rīḍwān* (beatitude, contentment) is 'greater' than Paradise—

⟪quran 9:72⟫ Allah has promised the believers and the believers a paradise from which rivers will flow for eternity in it and a good abode in the Garden of Eden... And our satisfaction from Allah is great... That is the great victory.

—it cannot but bestow joy upon the soul which effectively opens itself up to that Reality. In the words of Maulana Ali: 'Learning and reflecting upon knowledge is the delight of the knowers.' This is what one might call an 'epistemology of joy,' for 'the most joyous of mankind is the intellectual (*al-ʿāqil*).' This joyous epistemology—which flies in the face of modern scientific conceptions of knowledge as dry, cold and abstract—should be considered carefully, as it reveals a crucial dimension of the spiritual knowledge proper to the Imam's perspective, and indeed, to all spiritual traditions worthy of the name. Coming into contact with truths should impart happiness, and this, in the measure that the truths in question are fundamental to one's being or to Being as such. The two modes of being are ultimately identical, given the uniqueness or the 'one-and-onliness' of Being. That which has no second, no other, cannot enter the category of number; Being is indivisible. Hence, the being of the individual cannot be ultimately different from Being as such, however great the existential disparity—or rather, incommensurability—between the individual and Allah. True knowledge is knowledge of Being; thus it is perforce a kind of self-discovery, even if knowledge of the Absolute is impossible without absolute 'self'-abandonment. The highest discovery calls for the deepest effacement.

However, in the initial stages of this process, true knowledge acquired by the intellectual is a kind of self-discovery that imparts the happiness of a homecoming, a return from the exile of ignorance. This follows because 'the most intellectual of people is the one who is closest to Allah' and 'the one who is most alive.' To be close to Allah is to be fully alive, and this true life gives profound joy—the sheer joy of living. This is a joy proper to existence as such and is independent of particular experiences that may or may not be pleasurable.

Here again we see the crucial relationship between life and consciousness: the fruits of the 'in-spiration of the spirit of Allah' on one hand, and between this life of spiritual consciousness and joy on the other. Those who are true to the spirit of Allah within them truly possess joy. It is also by reason of the intimate and unshakeable certainty of the divine goodness and compassion that the true *faqīh*—not so much 'the jurist' as 'one who truly understands'—is defined by Maulana Ali as 'he who does not make people despair of the mercy of Allah, and does not make them lose hope in the gracious spirit of Allah (*rawḥ Allāh*).' Such is the sage's state of immersion in the joyous certainty of Allah's mercy that there is no place for any despair. It is a sign of disbelief to despair of this mercy:

⟪quran 15:56⟫ He said, "And who despairs of the mercy of his Lord, except those who are misguided."

⟪quran 6:54⟫ And if those of you who believe in Our Verse come to you, say, "Peace be upon you, your Lord has written upon Himself a mercy, for whoever of you does evil in ignorance, then repents after him, and reforms, for he is Oft-Forgiving, Most Merciful."

'My mercy encompasses all things' and 'Your Lord has prescribed for Himself mercy' are brought to life fully in the souls that have profound and not merely notional knowledge of the beatific nature of the divine Reality; they are the ones who truly 'understand.' They are the true *fuqahāʾ* (those possessing spiritual understanding).

## Beauty, Love, and the Completion of the Intellect

This feeling of joy, through intimate knowledge of the mercy of Allah, is inseparable from beauty. And the perception of this beauty is another natural association of the Intellect in Maulana Ali's perspective. The appreciation of beauty is not merely a question of subjective aesthetics; it is an objective aspect of the function of the Intellect. Beauty also takes one to the heart of ethics. The Arabic *iḥsān* is often simply translated as 'virtue' or 'excellence.' But one of its key literal meanings is 'making beautiful'—*ḥusn* means 'beauty.' Virtue, *iḥsān*, can thus be understood as that which 'makes the soul beautiful.' In this light, the relationship between the Intellect, ethics and aesthetics becomes more clearly discerned: 'The excellence of the Intellect,' says Maulana Ali, 'is in the beauty of things outward and inward' (⟪ar:جمال الظواہر والبواطن⟫). The outward beauty of forms and actions must be complemented by inward beauty—that is, beauty of soul, or virtue in the most integral and penetrating sense of the word. All of this follows naturally from two sayings attributed to the Prophet: 'Truly Allah is beautiful and He loves beauty' and 'Assume the character traits of Allah.'

Because Allah 'loves beauty,' this cultivation of 'beautiful traits' within oneself makes one lovable to Allah. This process of making oneself lovable to Allah is inseparable from the emulation of the virtues brought to perfection in the soul of the Prophet:

⟪quran 3:31⟫ Say: "If you love Allah, then follow me, Allah will love you and forgive you your sins, and Allah is Oft-Forgiving, Most Merciful."

This attraction of Allah's love through making oneself beautiful in the fullest sense is alluded to in another saying of Maulana Ali: 'The foundation of the Intellect is the assimilation of love' (⟪ar:أَوَّلُ العَقل التَّوَدُّدْ⟫). Rather than simply referring to love as such (⟪ar:وُد/مَوَدَّۃ⟫), Maulana Ali draws attention to the need to make oneself lovable to Allah by using the form *tawaddud*. This alludes to the process of integrating or realizing the principle of love within oneself, as well as expressing that love felt inside, outwardly. Love is thus appreciated both as outward radiance and inner attraction.

To 'follow' the Prophet and thus make oneself receptive to the divine influx of love means, among other things, cultivating the 'prophetic' virtues—virtues that are prophetic not in the sense that they belong exclusively to the Prophet, but because he is deemed to have realized virtue in all its plenitude, serving as an exemplar for his followers. It is thus not surprising to find that it is not only beauty and love that articulate the receptivity of the Intellect to the divine Reality, but also such qualities as kindness and contentment, courtesy, generosity and modesty, wise forbearance. None of these moral qualities are absent from the soul of the true intellectual. There can be no wisdom or Intellect in the fullest sense if the soul lacks these fundamental qualities. It is thanks to the presence of these virtues that the Intellect is able to develop those dimensions of depth and breadth without which it remains confined to the flat, horizontal plane of its own operations. Without virtue, the Intellect remains confined to the activity of reason alone, which, until touched by faith and deepened by grace, remains unaware of what lies beneath the surface of its own field of activity. It remains oblivious of the 'hidden treasures' of the Intellect. It remains blind to the need to 'integrate' the whole of the soul within the mould of that 'integral' or 'unifying' virtue which bestows integrity upon the intelligence. Such integration, or 'making one,' is absolutely fundamental to *tawhīd*. So *tawhīd* can now be understood as a dynamic process of **spiritual unification** that is demanded by the idea of the oneness of Allah. It is clearly necessary that truth must inspire the soul. But it is equally necessary that, conversely, virtue 'aerate' the Intellect, provide it with the 'leaven' (*khamīr*) which allows it to expand to its full dimensions.

Sinful, immoral qualities are grasped in this perspective not only as vices but also as so many blind spots or intellectual dysfunctions. The heart is 'diseased' by vice; its 'eye' is blinded by them and thus unable to 'see' the Real. As is said in the Qur'an:

⟪quran 2:10⟫ In their hearts is a disease, so God has increased them in sickness, and for them is a painful punishment, because they used to lie.

⟪quran 22:46⟫ It does not blind the sight, but it blinds the hearts in the chests.

As noted above, the vices are all comprised within egotism and this, in turn, is often described simply as 'caprice' (⟪ar:ھوی⟫). In one saying, Maulana Ali refers to this conquest over caprice as being tantamount to salvation: 'He attains deliverance whose Intellect dominates his caprice.' Deliverance—*falāḥ*, which comprises the sense both of salvation and beatitude (immense blessedness)—is thus, to some degree, accessible here and now. It is not just a grace granted after one's demise. This deliverance involves tasting the joy which emanates from certainty, from intimate contact with the spirit of this certainty. As to the means of cultivating receptivity to this spirit of certainty, what is required, over and above obedience to the rules and regulations of the revealed Law, is that combination of purity of intention and sincerity of devotion which is expressed by the word *ikhlāṣ*. Without this sincerity or purity, complying with those formal rules is nothing but hypocrisy (*nifāq*) and pretension (*riyāʾ*)—which, in turn, is defined by Maulana Ali as the gravest of all sins, *shirk* (polytheism), the attribution of 'partners' to Allah: 'Know that the slightest pretension (*riyāʾ*) is polytheism.' This sentence can be taken as a comment upon one of the most important condemnations of hypocrisy found in the Quran:

⟪quran 107:1-7⟫ Have you seen him who denies the Recompense? / That is he who repulses the orphan, / And urges not the feeding of the poor... / So woe unto those performers of prayer, / Who delay their prayer from their stated fixed times, / Those who do good deeds only to be seen, / And refuse the small kindness.

This short sura makes it clear that to be 'religious' in one's formal acts of devotion has no value whatsoever unless these acts be integrated into a life of virtue and unless one is performing one's devotion with the right intention—that is, with *ikhlāṣ*. It also helps us to see the reason for not referring to Maulana Ali's ethos as simply 'religious' but as 'spiritual,' taking the ideals of religion to their most profound application. Without this dimension of depth, religion is meaningless.

## The Intellect and the Foundation of Religion

'There is no religion for one who has no Intellect' says Maulana Ali.[^cite-90] Likewise, 'Action without knowledge is error.' The intention motivating the action is what counts. For 'action—all action—is dust, except what is purified [accomplished sincerely] within it.' This detachment from action as such and the orientation instead toward an inner state of being, one from which good actions cannot but emerge, clearly reflects what is found in the Quran:

⟪quran 22:37⟫ Allah will not receive its flesh or its blood, but He will receive it from you, and He will also make it available to you, so that you may be proud of what Allah has guided you, and give good tidings to the good people.

One might argue that no group better exemplified the principle expressed in this saying than the *Khārijites* (literally, 'those who go out'). Among this group that rebelled against Maulana Ali were numerous 'readers' (*qurrāʾ*, sing. *qāriʾ*) of the Quran—people who possessed considerable religious prestige on account of their knowledge of the Quran and their intense worship. Yet their piety, however outwardly impressive, was more apparent than real. It was in fact a 'zeal of bitterness,' an imitation of true piety. When his supporters made mention of the long night vigils performed by members of this group, Maulana Ali gave this sharp reply: 'Sleeping with certainty is better than praying with doubt.'[^cite-91] Certainty, as we have seen, imparts peace and joy. Thus sleep, for one who is immersed in what Maulana Ali calls 'the spirit of certainty,' is a bodily repose which further deepens the happiness that flows from a good conscience and spiritual certitude. This spiritual certitude is understood as a kind of permanent 'wakefulness,' in the sense of being awake to the higher realities. Thus, bodily sleep does not contradict spiritual wakefulness. As the hadith of the Prophet states, 'My eyes sleep, but my heart sleeps not.'

In relation to formal worship, the true intellectual is one who is obedient to the divine injunction to pray. But rather than praying in mechanical fashion and believing that the action alone suffices, the *ʿāqil* knows that such obedience is a necessary but not sufficient condition for achieving receptivity to grace. There must be consciousness of what one is doing and why one is doing it. And it is this awareness, together with virtue or 'inward beauty,' that attracts divine acceptance. 'The most worthy knowledge for you is that by virtue of whose presence, action is accepted by Allah,' says Maulana Ali.

## They Make Easy What the Extravagant Find Harsh

The next sentence now takes us, among other things, to the impact made on the soul of the true sages by recollection of the inevitability of death in this world and the consequent necessity of renunciation within it. In the first place, that which is found to be 'harsh' by those who are extravagant—the avid, worldly types—is outward adversity, difficulty, deprivation. These are all circumstances which, in the Quran, are viewed as the trials which inevitably accompany life in this world:

⟪quran 2:155-157⟫ And certainly, We shall test you with something of fear, hunger, loss of wealth, lives and fruits, but give glad tidings to the patient ones. / Who, when afflicted with calamity, say: "Truly! To Allah we belong and truly, to Him we shall return." / They are those on whom are the blessings from their Lord, and receive His Mercy, and it is they who are the guided-ones.

The wise, the rightly-guided (*al-muhtadūn*), perceive the 'blessings' that are showered down upon those who are patient in the face of afflictions. This patience being a result of the completeness of their knowledge of the truth:

⟪quran 103:1-3⟫ By the age / Verily! Man is in loss, / Except those who believe and do righteous good deeds, and recommend one another to the truth.

These are the believers who remain steadfast in the face of trials, being firmly rooted in their awareness of their origin and end, and the fact that 'unto Him we are ever-returning.' For this reason, they can 'make easy' the trials and difficulties of life, whereas the 'extravagant' experience them solely in their aspect of harshness and bitterness. The 'ease' that permanently exists in all difficulty is hidden from the ignorant but discerned by the wise:

⟪quran 94:5-6⟫ So with hardship easy / with hardship easy.

The wise go even further than simply anticipating 'ease' as the inevitable fruit of hardship. They taste this fruit already as the seed hidden within hardship itself, for they actually 'befriend that by which the ignorant are estranged.'

We can understand one level of meaning here with the help of this saying of Maulana Ali: 'Death is more intimate to me than the breast of the mother is to the suckling baby.' For in the verse cited above, the mention of trials and difficulties is preceded by a reminder that those who are slain 'in the path of Allah' are not really dead—'No! they are alive but you do not perceive.' We have here not simply an affirmation of the life hereafter but also an allusion to the esoteric meaning of being 'slain' for Allah—⟪ar:الفنا فی اللہ⟫ (annihilation, or effacement in Allah). This intimacy with death, mentioned by Maulana Ali, can be seen to relate to this 'death' while still alive, which he also alludes to in another saying: 'I am the death of the dead.' It is because life in the world itself is a kind of death—a limitation. This life is privation (a state in which things that are essential for human well-being are lacking), in contrast to the infinite abundance of the divine realms. Therefore, death in this world is tantamount to true life. As the Quran says:

⟪quran 29:64⟫ And this life of this world is nothing but a pastime and a play, and the abode of the Hereafter is for the beast, if they only knew.

However, it is important to stress that it is not the physical world as such that is to be negated, but rather worldliness. It is the egotistic attachment to this world that must be overcome. Here, we need to dwell a little on one of the key principles of Maulana Ali's perspective, which is stressed repeatedly throughout the *Nahj al-Balāgha*. This is the principle of *zuhd*, which cannot be translated by any single word in English. It comprises several meanings: Asceticism, Austerity, Abstention, Detachment, Doing without, Making little of. The following passage from the *Nahj al-Balāgha* expresses well the attitude of the *zāhid* (the ascetic) toward this world:

> The world is an abode for which annihilation is ordained, and for its people departure from it is decreed. It is sweet and green, and it hastens to one who seeks it. It confounds the heart that gazes upon it. So set out from it with the best of the provision available to you, and ask not from it above what suffices and seek not from it more than what can be attained.

What is this 'best of provision' (*zād*)? In many places in the *Nahj*, it is described as *taqwā*—another word difficult to translate. The meaning of *taqwā* is: Piety, Fear of Allah, Awe, and the consciousness of Allah. 'I strongly encourage you, O slaves of Allah, to *taqwā*, which is the true provision,' says Maulana Ali. This provision for the next world is only attained in the measure of one's detachment from the perishable aspects of the 'life of this world.' The Quran states:

⟪quran 17:18⟫ Whoever desires hastening, We hasten for him in it what we will, for whom We will, then We have made for him Hell to pray for him, reprehensible, praised, and praised.

The contemptible aspect of this world is described in vivid terms by Maulana Ali:

- By Allah, this world of yours is more contemptible in my eyes than a chewed-up bone of a pig in the hand of a leper
- The image of this world is that of a snake—soft to touch but filled with virulent poison
- This world is 'as worthless to me as that which drips from the nose of a goat.'

It is the grandeur and beauty of Allah that render this world insignificant and distasteful. It is only in the light of the Absolute that the pleasures and attractions of the world appear fleeting and illusory: 'The greatness of the Creator for you belittles creatures in your view.' In similar vein, Maulana Ali refers to a 'brother in Allah': 'What made him great in my eyes was the smallness of the world in his eyes.'

This, according to Quranic usage of *zuhd*, is the real meaning of *zuhd*. In Surah Yusuf, the brothers of Yusuf, 'deemed him of little value' in selling him for a small price:

⟪quran 12:20⟫ And they bought him for a price of 2 dirhams, and they were among the ascetics.

They were described as being, in relation to him, '*zāhidīn*' (those who regard as insignificant). The world, in Maulana Ali's perspective, is likewise deemed of little value. Not that it is altogether valueless, but that, in relation to the Hereafter, in relation to eternity, in relation to the ultimate Reality, it is rendered all but insignificant. All created things are reduced to infinitesimal, fleeting shadows, destined for annihilation:

⟪quran 28:88⟫ All things perished except his face, to him is the judgment, and to him you shall return.

However, the world is not deprived of all value:

⟪quran 17:44⟫ The seven heavens and the earth and those in them praise Him, and there is nothing but praise Him, but you do not understand their praise, for He was Forgiving, Forgiving.

Everything in the world, all the creatures in existence, thus proclaim the infinite creativity of the Creator. To help us imaginatively comprehend the mystery of this universal praise, the Quran tells us:

⟪quran 24:41⟫ Have you not seen that Allah praises Him of those who are in the heavens, the earth, and the birds?

It is thus not at all contradictory for Maulana Ali to both despise the world and yet express in his descriptions the beauties of virgin nature, which includes that unsurpassable marvel, the peacock. This comes in a sermon which includes descriptions of birds in general, mentioning also ants, serpents and elephants.

It is noteworthy that the sermon concludes with an exciting description of Paradise, comparing the marvels of this world with the delights of the next. When compared to the latter, all terrestrial phenomena pale into insignificance. For once the 'eye of the heart' is directed to Paradise, 'your soul would become averse to the marvels of this world—its passions, its pleasures and its embellished scenery.' It might seem contradictory to bring together such apparently opposed qualities as joy in certainty, discussed above, and the 'befriending' of hardship. But the latter is in fact the fruit of the former. All difficulties without exception are endured, thanks to the spiritual resources that are made available by faith combined with intellectual assimilation—faith that becomes, in the measure it is intellectually assimilated, *maʿrifat* (gnosis, direct knowledge). This gnosis comprises a certainty so deep and so complete that 'even if the veil be lifted' it cannot increase, according to the famous saying of Maulana Ali: ⟪ar:لو کشف الغطاء ما ازدت یقینا⟫—'Even if all the veils of this world were lifted from my eyes, it would not increase my certainty in the least bit.'[^cite-92]

It is possible to believe, at one level of the mind, that 'there is no deity but Allah.' Maulana Ali, however, draws attention to what this apparently simple belief should imply when it is 'sincere' or 'pure': 'I bear witness that there is no deity but Allah alone Who has no partner—a testimony whose sincerity has been tested and whose essence has been assimilated with conviction.' (⟪ar:مُعتَقَداً مُصَعصَھَا⟫). The word *muṣāṣ* refers to the choicest and purest part of any substance; that which constitutes its essence. The root of the word *maṣṣa* means 'to suck.' The idea here is that the liquid or juice of a substance or fruit contains its essence, that which is 'sucked' out of it. In the present context, Maulana Ali is stressing the spiritual substance of his testimony, as opposed to mere verbal affirmation or mental comprehension of the words. He affirms a heartfelt connection with the One for whom his testimony bears witness. The words defining the testimony echo its reality. The words are not to be confused with that reality itself. He continues: 'We cling to it always, for as long as we endure, storing it up as protection against the terrors that will befall us. For truly, it renders faith resolute, opens up spiritual virtue, pleases al-Raḥmān (the All-Compassionate), repels al-shayṭān (the devil).'

Going back to '**befriending** that by which the ignorant are estranged,' one might say that this implies not just the trials of this life, which are a source of strength for the wise and repulsion for the ignorant, but also the inescapability of death. For the wise, this encounter with Allah for a blissful eternity means a release. In other words, it constitutes a mode of *Tawhīd*, whereby the soul enjoys ever greater degrees of integration within higher principles. For the ignorant, conversely, death is perceived as a frightful disintegration. It means the end of their world rather than the beginning of an endless life. This goes back to the earlier concept of death while still alive, described as *fanāʾ* (extinction, effacement), the 'perishing' of egoistic consciousness in the face of the Absolute. The ignorant shrinks in horror at the prospect of the loss of what is falsely taken to be 'their' life. They do not realize that this sacrifice of egoistic and limited self-consciousness is the very basis on which infinite consciousness is glimpsed or 'tasted.' One can only 'taste' the ocean of the Real by being drowned in it. One could even say that the means to this ultimate attainment—the act of pure prayer, total concentration on the Real—is also 'embraced' by the wise and feared by the ignorant. For this concentration strictly precludes all worldly or egotistic preoccupations. The very intensity of the concentration upon the Absolute burns away thoughts of the relative. For those who ignorantly identify with relativities, both inward and outward, this implies a kind of death. For the wise, conversely, this concentration on the Absolute—the unsullied 'remembrance of Allah'—is a joyous contemplation of reality before which the ego is displaced and all illusions are dispelled.

## With Their Bodies, They Keep Company With the World, While Their Spirits Are Tied to the Transcendent Realm

This sentence can be more concretely understood if it is read in the light of another well-known expression of Maulana Ali. He describes himself as belonging to a group whose degree of spirituality is such that 'their hearts are in the Gardens of Paradise while their bodies are at work.' The fact of being 'tied' or 'attached' to that transcendent realm, consisting of the paradisal abodes, right up to the divine Presence itself, implies then that they are already 'within' that transcendent realm more than they are in this earthly one. This helps explain the saying quoted earlier: Allah keeps man perpetually in that which his soul desires.
