Those whose hearts or spirits are 'in' the transcendent domain are those whose consciousness is substantially (and not merely conceptually) determined by the transcendent realities. Those whose consciousness is determined by worldly matters are 'in' this world in the sense of being enclosed by its formal limitations. Their orientation and imagination are trapped within a worldly framework. Their actions bear the mark of this 'worldliness,' whereas the actions of the wise are the fruits of their immersion in a higher reality. One might say that their outward actions in the world disguise their inward transcendence of all action. They ACT, but are not bound by the fruits of their action. As stated earlier, they are aware that 'action—all action—is dust, except what is purified (accomplished sincerely) within.'

## The Hereafter as Spiritual Reality

At this point it would be appropriate to dwell briefly on the role played by the notion of the 'Hereafter' within the ethical framework of Maulana Ali. As we saw earlier and will see in what follows, the Hereafter is repeatedly invoked or evoked in the discourses of Maulana Ali. Is it merely a question of offering a heavenly carrot and threatening a hellish stick in order simply to 'enjoin the good and forbid the evil'? Even if we believe that the answer is affirmative on one level, there is a deeper side to this seemingly simplistic utilization of the ethical import of divine warnings and promises on human action.

The point of view adopted in regard to ethical orientation here is defined essentially by the spiritual reality not just of Allah but also of the Hereafter—both objectively, in itself, and subjectively, that is, insofar as it is assimilated by the individual. It thus becomes a dynamic dimension, a process, orientation or reflux within the very soul of the believer, rather than a mere belief or hope. Anticipation of the Hereafter is one thing; but its realization is completely another. And it is this realization that characterizes the soul of the 'friend' of Allah (*walī Allāh*), the saint and the sage.[^cite-93]

As noted earlier, Maulana Ali says in a famous sentence which bears witness to his state of total immersion within the reality of which he is certain: 'Were the veil of this world to be removed, I would not increase in certitude' (⟪ar:لو کشف الغطاء ما ازدت یقینا⟫). The anticipation or foretaste of the Hereafter is deepened in proportion to one's virtue, piety and sanctity. It does not become merely a heuristic tool whose value is restricted to its impact on moral action and thus rendered superfluous once this impact is achieved. In this connection, the following moving passage from the *Nahj* deserves reflection:

> If you were to cast the eye of your heart towards what is described for you of the Garden, your soul would become averse to the marvels of this world—its passions, its pleasures and its embellished scenery. And your soul would be mesmerized in contemplation of the swaying trees whose roots lie hidden in dunes of musk on the banks of the rivers of the Garden, and the clusters of dazzling pearls hanging down from the branches of its trees.

It is, of course, true that the highest degree of spirituality transcends the desire and moral conduct for Paradise and the fear of Hell. But this means that when such higher degrees of realization have been attained, the state of the soul is one that can properly be characterized as 'paradisal'—that is, as being already so utterly content with the beatific presence of Allah that it can desire nothing more. This state of soul is called in the Quran *Nafs al-Muṭmaʾinna*:

⟪quran 89:27⟫ O the reassuring soul.

Maulana Ali frequently echoes, implicitly if not explicitly, the Quranic principle that the 'Hereafter is better and more lasting':

⟪quran 87:17⟫ And the hereafter is better and the last.

The Hereafter is not only the 'time' when divine warnings and promises are consummated; it is also the 'space' within which all positive qualities—human, angelic, divine—are manifested in their absolute nature. These qualities are glimpsed on this earth as reflections of the higher realities, which encompass and subtly penetrate the entire sphere of terrestrial existence. The Quran, perhaps more so than any other scripture, presents to the imagination numerous and complex descriptions of paradisal bliss. One of the key functions of this is alluded to in the Quran itself:

⟪quran 38:46⟫ We will save them purely anniversary of the house.

The mere thought of Paradise is in itself a purification of the mind and heart. It is a means of averting from the soul the ever-present temptation to seek its ultimate happiness and well-being in this world alone. On the contrary, one's well-being in this world, in regard to action, lies in the performance of good deeds, and such well-being is proportioned to the depth of one's belief that:

⟪quran 73:20⟫ And whatever good you offer yourselves, you will find it with Allah the best and the greatest reward.

In other words, a sincere orientation towards the Hereafter is transformed into a perception of heavenly beatitude already in the here-below, in the form precisely of those good acts and attitudes which are of one and the same nature as that felicity to which they lead. In this light, morally good actions and noble intentions are grasped by one who sees beyond the veil of this world as vivid prefigurations of heavenly realities, and not just as ethical prerequisites for salvation. Goodness, in other words, does not just lead to a heavenly reward; it already is something of this reward. As the Quran asks:

⟪quran 55:60⟫ Is the part of the best except the best.

This is echoed in Maulana Ali's saying, 'The fruit of good action is like its root.' This statement can be seen as echoing the idea that the end is already present in the means leading to it. Finally, let us quote the following verse of the Quran, which is a fitting way to conclude this topic and introduce the next:

⟪quran 2:25⟫ And those who believed and did good deeds gave good tidings that they will have a Garden under which rivers flow, and whenever they get their livelihood from it, they say, "This is what we have provided before, and we have come to it, similar to it."

The 'fruits' of the paradisal gardens are thus experienced already in this world in the form of all goodness, beauty and truth and the diverse modes of happiness flowing therefrom. All such experiences are so many foretastes of the ultimate blessings in Paradise. On the ethical plane, the performance of good deeds and even more directly, the realization of intrinsic virtue—*iḥsān*—is thus not simply a prerequisite for future salvation, but is already a kind of present deliverance. It is a deliverance from the imprisonment of sin on the outward plane and from the bondage of egocentricity on the inward plane. This theme of salvation here and now, grounded in unshakeable certitude, is fundamental to the spirit of Maulana Ali's teachings.
